Sculptures in the <a href="/wiki/Loggia_dei_Lanzi" title="Loggia dei Lanzi">Loggia dei Lanzi</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%27David%27_by_Michelangelo_JBU05.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/%27David%27_by_Michelangelo_JBU05.JPG/220px-%27David%27_by_Michelangelo_JBU05.JPG" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/%27David%27_by_Michelangelo_JBU05.JPG/330px-%27David%27_by_Michelangelo_JBU05.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/%27David%27_by_Michelangelo_JBU05.JPG/440px-%27David%27_by_Michelangelo_JBU05.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2215" data-file-height="3323" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%27David%27_by_Michelangelo_JBU05.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Michelangelo's <i><a href="/wiki/David_(Michelangelo)" title="David (Michelangelo)">David</a></i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cimabue" title="Cimabue">Cimabue</a> and <a href="/wiki/Giotto_di_Bondone" class="mw-redirect" title="Giotto di Bondone">Giotto</a>, the fathers of Italian painting, lived in Florence as well as Arnolfo and <a href="/wiki/Andrea_Pisano" title="Andrea Pisano">Andrea Pisano</a>, renewers of architecture and sculpture; Brunelleschi, <a href="/wiki/Donatello" title="Donatello">Donatello</a> and Masaccio, forefathers of the Renaissance, Ghiberti and the Della Robbias, <a href="/wiki/Filippo_Lippi" title="Filippo Lippi">Filippo Lippi</a> and Angelico; Botticelli, Paolo Uccello and the universal genius of Leonardo da Vinci and Michelangelo.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>Their works, together with those of many other generations of artists, are gathered in the several museums of the town: the Uffizi Gallery, the Palatina gallery with the paintings of the "Golden Ages",<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Bargello" title="Bargello">Bargello</a> with the sculptures of the Renaissance, the museum of San Marco with <a href="/wiki/Fra_Angelico" title="Fra Angelico">Fra Angelico</a>'s works, the Academy, the chapels of the Medicis<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Buonarroti's house with the sculptures of Michelangelo, the following museums: Bardini, Horne, Stibbert, Romano, Corsini, The Gallery of Modern Art, the <a href="/wiki/Museo_dell%27Opera_del_Duomo_(Florence)" title="Museo dell'Opera del Duomo (Florence)">Museo dell'Opera del Duomo</a>, the museum of Silverware and the museum of <a href="/wiki/Gemstone" title="Gemstone">Precious Stones</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Several monuments are located in Florence: the Florence Baptistery with its mosaics; the Cathedral with its sculptures, the medieval churches with bands of frescoes; public as well as private palaces: <a href="/wiki/Palazzo_Vecchio" title="Palazzo Vecchio">Palazzo Vecchio</a>, Palazzo Pitti, <a href="/wiki/Palazzo_Medici_Riccardi" title="Palazzo Medici Riccardi">Palazzo Medici Riccardi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palazzo_Davanzati" title="Palazzo Davanzati">Palazzo Davanzati</a>; monasteries, cloisters, refectories; the "Certosa". In the archaeological museum includes documents of Etruscan civilisation.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> In fact the city is so rich in art that some first time visitors experience the <a href="/wiki/Stendhal_syndrome" title="Stendhal syndrome">Stendhal syndrome</a> as they encounter its art for the first time.<sup id="cite_ref-ncjqkw_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ncjqkw-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Uffizi_Hallway.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Uffizi_Hallway.jpg/220px-Uffizi_Hallway.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Uffizi_Hallway.jpg/330px-Uffizi_Hallway.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Uffizi_Hallway.jpg/440px-Uffizi_Hallway.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Uffizi_Hallway.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Uffizi" title="Uffizi">Uffizi</a> hallway</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Florentine architects such as Filippo Brunelleschi (1377–1466) and Leon Battista Alberti (1404–1472) were among the fathers of both Renaissance and <a href="/wiki/Neoclassical_architecture" title="Neoclassical architecture">Neoclassical architecture</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>The cathedral, topped by Brunelleschi's dome, dominates the Florentine skyline. The Florentines decided to start building it – late in the 13th century, without a design for the dome. The project proposed by Brunelleschi in the 14th century was the largest ever built at the time, and the first major dome built in Europe since the two great domes of Roman times – the <a href="/wiki/Pantheon,_Rome" title="Pantheon, Rome">Pantheon</a> in Rome, and <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a> in <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>. The dome of Santa Maria del Fiore remains the largest brick construction of its kind in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> In front of it is the medieval Baptistery. The two buildings incorporate in their decoration the transition from the Middle Ages to the Renaissance. In recent years, most of the important works of art from the two buildings – and from the nearby <a href="/wiki/Giotto%27s_Campanile" title="Giotto's Campanile">Giotto's Campanile</a>, have been removed and replaced by copies. The originals are now housed in the Museum dell'Opera del Duomo, just to the east of the Cathedral.</p>
<p>Florence has large numbers of art-filled churches, such as San Miniato al Monte, San Lorenzo, Santa Maria Novella, Santa Trinita, Santa Maria del Carmine, Santa Croce, Santo Spirito, the Annunziata, Ognissanti and numerous others.<sup id="cite_ref-britannica.com_8-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britannica.com-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Firenze_Palazzo_della_Signoria,_better_known_as_the_Palazzo_Vecchio.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Firenze_Palazzo_della_Signoria%2C_better_known_as_the_Palazzo_Vecchio.jpg/170px-Firenze_Palazzo_della_Signoria%2C_better_known_as_the_Palazzo_Vecchio.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Firenze_Palazzo_della_Signoria%2C_better_known_as_the_Palazzo_Vecchio.jpg/255px-Firenze_Palazzo_della_Signoria%2C_better_known_as_the_Palazzo_Vecchio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Firenze_Palazzo_della_Signoria%2C_better_known_as_the_Palazzo_Vecchio.jpg/340px-Firenze_Palazzo_della_Signoria%2C_better_known_as_the_Palazzo_Vecchio.jpg 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="1280" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Firenze_Palazzo_della_Signoria,_better_known_as_the_Palazzo_Vecchio.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Palazzo della Signoria, better known as the Palazzo Vecchio (English: The Old Palace)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Artists associated with Florence range from <a href="/wiki/Arnolfo_di_Cambio" title="Arnolfo di Cambio">Arnolfo di Cambio</a> and Cimabue to Giotto, Nanni di Banco, and Paolo Uccello; through Lorenzo Ghiberti, and Donatello and Massaccio and the della Robbia family; through Fra Angelico and Botticelli and Piero della Francesca, and on to Michelangelo and Leonardo da Vinci. Others include Benvenuto Cellini, Andrea del Sarto, Benozzo Gozzoli, Domenico Ghirlandaio, Filippo Lippi, Bernardo Buontalenti, Orcagna, Pollaiuolo, Filippino Lippi, Verrocchio, Bronzino, Desiderio da Settignano, Michelozzo, the Rossellis, the Sangallos, and Pontormo. Artists from other regions who worked in Florence include Raphael, Andrea Pisano, Giambologna, Il Sodoma and Peter Paul Rubens.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dome_of_Cattedrale_di_Santa_Maria_del_Fiore_(Florence).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Dome_of_Cattedrale_di_Santa_Maria_del_Fiore_%28Florence%29.jpg/220px-Dome_of_Cattedrale_di_Santa_Maria_del_Fiore_%28Florence%29.jpg" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Dome_of_Cattedrale_di_Santa_Maria_del_Fiore_%28Florence%29.jpg/330px-Dome_of_Cattedrale_di_Santa_Maria_del_Fiore_%28Florence%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Dome_of_Cattedrale_di_Santa_Maria_del_Fiore_%28Florence%29.jpg/440px-Dome_of_Cattedrale_di_Santa_Maria_del_Fiore_%28Florence%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4516" data-file-height="3310" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dome_of_Cattedrale_di_Santa_Maria_del_Fiore_(Florence).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Brunelleschi's dome</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Picture galleries in Florence include the Uffizi and the Pitti Palace. Two superb collections of sculpture are in the Bargello and the Museum of the Works of the Duomo. They are filled with the creations of Donatello, Verrochio, Desiderio da Settignano, Michelangelo and others. The Galleria dell'Accademia has Michelangelo's David – perhaps the best-known work of art anywhere, plus the unfinished statues of the slaves Michelangelo created for the tomb of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Julius_II" title="Pope Julius II">Pope Julius II</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Other sights include the medieval city hall, the Palazzo della Signoria (also known as the Palazzo Vecchio), the <a href="/wiki/National_Archaeological_Museum_(Florence)" title="National Archaeological Museum (Florence)">Archeological Museum</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Museo_Galileo" title="Museo Galileo">Museum of the History of Science</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Garden_of_Archimedes" title="Garden of Archimedes">Garden of Archimedes</a>, the Palazzo Davanzatti, the Stibbert Museum, St. Marks, the Medici Chapels, the Museum of the Works of Santa Croce, the Museum of the Cloister of Santa Maria Novella, the Zoological Museum ("<a href="/wiki/La_Specola" title="La Specola">La Specola</a>"), the Bardini, and the Museo Horne. There is also a collection of works by the modern sculptor, <a href="/wiki/Marino_Marini_(sculptor)" title="Marino Marini (sculptor)">Marino Marini</a>, in a museum named after him. The Strozzi Palace is the site of special exhibits.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Florence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Florentine_dialect" title="Florentine dialect">Florentine dialect</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Tuscan_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Tuscan language">Tuscan language</a></div>
<p>Florentine (<i>fiorentino</i>), spoken by inhabitants of Florence and its environs, is a <a href="/wiki/Tuscan_Language" class="mw-redirect" title="Tuscan Language">Tuscan dialect</a> and the immediate <a href="/wiki/Proto-language" title="Proto-language">parent language</a> to modern Italian.</p>
<p>Its vocabulary and pronunciation are largely identical to standard Italian, though the hard <i>c</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[k]</span> between two vowels (as in <i>ducato</i>) is pronounced as a <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_glottal_fricative" title="Voiceless glottal fricative">fricative</a> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[h]</span>, similar to an English <i>h</i>. This gives Florentines a highly recognisable accent (the so-called <a href="/wiki/Tuscan_gorgia" title="Tuscan gorgia">gorgia toscana</a>). Other traits include using a form of the <a href="/wiki/Subjunctive_mood" title="Subjunctive mood">subjunctive mood</a> last commonly used in <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Times" title="Medieval Times">medieval times</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> a frequent usage in everyday speech of the modern subjunctive, and a shortened pronunciation of the <a href="/wiki/Article_(grammar)" title="Article (grammar)">definite article</a>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[i]</span> instead of "il".</p>
<p>Dante, Petrarch, and Boccaccio pioneered the use of the vernacular<sup id="cite_ref-florenceholidays.com_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-florenceholidays.com-71">[71]</a></sup> instead of the Latin used for most literary works at the time.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Florence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Decamaron_intro.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Decamaron_intro.jpg/220px-Decamaron_intro.jpg" width="220" height="230" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Decamaron_intro.jpg/330px-Decamaron_intro.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Decamaron_intro.jpg/440px-Decamaron_intro.jpg 2x" data-file-width="538" data-file-height="562" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Decamaron_intro.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The introduction of the <i>Decameron</i> (1350–1353) by <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Boccaccio" title="Giovanni Boccaccio">Giovanni Boccaccio</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Despite Latin being the main language of the courts and the Church in the Middle Ages, writers such as Dante Alighieri<sup id="cite_ref-florenceholidays.com_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-florenceholidays.com-71">[71]</a></sup> and many others used their own language, the Florentine vernacular descended from Latin, in composing their greatest works. The oldest literary pieces written in Florentine go as far back as the 13th century. Florence's literature fully blossomed in the 14th century, when not only Dante with his <i><a href="/wiki/Divine_Comedy" title="Divine Comedy">Divine Comedy</a></i> (1306–1321) and Petrarch, but also poets such as <a href="/wiki/Guido_Cavalcanti" title="Guido Cavalcanti">Guido Cavalcanti</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lapo_Gianni" title="Lapo Gianni">Lapo Gianni</a> composed their most important works.<sup id="cite_ref-florenceholidays.com_71-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-florenceholidays.com-71">[71]</a></sup> Dante's masterpiece is the <i>Divine Comedy</i>, which mainly deals with the poet himself taking an allegoric and moral tour of Hell, Purgatory and finally Heaven, during which he meets numerous mythological or real characters of his age or before. He is first guided by the Roman poet <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a>, whose non-Christian beliefs damned him to Hell. Later on he is joined by <a href="/wiki/Divine_Comedy" title="Divine Comedy">Beatrice</a>, who guides him through Heaven.<sup id="cite_ref-florenceholidays.com_71-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-florenceholidays.com-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 14th century, <a href="/wiki/Petrarch" title="Petrarch">Petrarch</a><sup id="cite_ref-florenceholidays_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-florenceholidays-72">[72]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Boccaccio" title="Giovanni Boccaccio">Giovanni Boccaccio</a><sup id="cite_ref-florenceholidays_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-florenceholidays-72">[72]</a></sup> led the literary scene in Florence after Dante's death in 1321. Petrarch was an all-rounder writer, author and poet, but was particularly known for his <i><a href="/wiki/Il_Canzoniere" title="Il Canzoniere">Canzoniere</a></i>, or the Book of Songs, where he conveyed his unremitting love for Laura.<sup id="cite_ref-florenceholidays_72-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-florenceholidays-72">[72]</a></sup> His style of writing has since become known as <i>Petrarchism</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-florenceholidays_72-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-florenceholidays-72">[72]</a></sup> Boccaccio was better known for his <i><a href="/wiki/The_Decameron" title="The Decameron">Decameron</a></i>, a slightly grim story of Florence during the 1350s bubonic plague, known as the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a>, when some people fled the ravaged city to an isolated country mansion, and spent their time there recounting stories and novellas taken from the medieval and contemporary tradition. All of this is written in a series of 100 distinct novellas.<sup id="cite_ref-florenceholidays_72-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-florenceholidays-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 16th century, during the Renaissance, Florence was the home town of political writer and philosopher Niccolò Machiavelli, whose ideas on how rulers should govern the land, detailed in <i>The Prince</i>, spread across European courts and enjoyed enduring popularity for centuries. These principles became known as <i><a href="/wiki/Machiavellianism" title="Machiavellianism">Machiavellianism</a></i>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Florence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Florence" title="Music of Florence">Music of Florence</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Tuscany" title="Music of Tuscany">Music of Tuscany</a></div>
<p>Florence became a musical centre during the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> and music and the performing arts remain an important part of its culture. During the Renaissance there were four kinds of musical patronage in the city with respect to both sacred and secular music: state, corporate, church, and private.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> and it was here that the <a href="/wiki/Florentine_Camerata" title="Florentine Camerata">Florentine Camerata</a> convened in the mid-16th century and experimented with setting tales of Greek mythology to music and staging the result—in other words, the first operas, setting the wheels in motion not just for the further development of the operatic form, but for later developments of separate "classical" forms such as the symphony.</p>
<p>Opera was invented in Florence in the late 16th century.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>Composers and musicians who have lived in Florence include <a href="/wiki/Piero_Strozzi_(composer)" title="Piero Strozzi (composer)">Piero Strozzi</a> (1550 – after 1608), <a href="/wiki/Giulio_Caccini" title="Giulio Caccini">Giulio Caccini</a> (1551–1618) and <a href="/wiki/Mike_Francis_(musician)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mike Francis (musician)">Mike Francis</a> (1961–2009).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cinema">Cinema</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Florence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Cinema">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Florence has been a setting for numerous works of fiction and movies, including the novels and associated films, such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Light_in_the_Piazza_(novel)" title="The Light in the Piazza (novel)">Light in the Piazza</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Calmi_Cuori_Appassionati" title="Calmi Cuori Appassionati">Calmi Cuori Appassionati</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Hannibal_(film)" title="Hannibal (film)">Hannibal</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/A_Room_with_a_View_(1986_film)" class="mw-redirect" title="A Room with a View (1986 film)">A Room with a View</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Tea_with_Mussolini" title="Tea with Mussolini">Tea with Mussolini</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Virgin_Territory" title="Virgin Territory">Virgin Territory</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Inferno_(2016_film)" title="Inferno (2016 film)">Inferno</a></i>. The city is home to renowned Italian actors and actresses, such as <a href="/wiki/Roberto_Benigni" title="Roberto Benigni">Roberto Benigni</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_Pieraccioni" title="Leonardo Pieraccioni">Leonardo Pieraccioni</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vittoria_Puccini" title="Vittoria Puccini">Vittoria Puccini</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">Cuisine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Florence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Cuisine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bistecca_alla_fiorentina-01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Bistecca_alla_fiorentina-01.jpg/220px-Bistecca_alla_fiorentina-01.jpg" width="220" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Bistecca_alla_fiorentina-01.jpg/330px-Bistecca_alla_fiorentina-01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Bistecca_alla_fiorentina-01.jpg/440px-Bistecca_alla_fiorentina-01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="875" data-file-height="678" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bistecca_alla_fiorentina-01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Florentine steak in Florence.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Florentine food grows out of a tradition of peasant eating rather than rarefied high cooking. The majority of dishes are based on meat. The whole animal was traditionally eaten; <a href="/wiki/Tripe" title="Tripe">tripe</a> (<i>trippa</i>) and stomach (<i><a href="/wiki/Lampredotto" title="Lampredotto">lampredotto</a></i>) were once regularly on the menu and still are sold at the food carts stationed throughout the city. <a href="/wiki/Antipasto" title="Antipasto">Antipasti</a> include <i>crostini toscani</i>, sliced bread rounds topped with a chicken liver-based <a href="/wiki/P%C3%A2t%C3%A9" title="Pâté">pâté</a>, and sliced meats (mainly <a href="/wiki/Prosciutto" title="Prosciutto">prosciutto</a> and <a href="/wiki/Salame" class="mw-redirect" title="Salame">salame</a>, often served with melon when in season). The typically saltless Tuscan bread, obtained with natural <a href="/wiki/Levain" class="mw-redirect" title="Levain">levain</a> frequently features in Florentine courses, especially in its soups, <i><a href="/wiki/Ribollita" title="Ribollita">ribollita</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Pappa_al_pomodoro" title="Pappa al pomodoro">pappa al pomodoro</a></i>, or in the salad of bread and fresh vegetables called <i><a href="/wiki/Panzanella" title="Panzanella">panzanella</a></i> that is served in summer. The <i><a href="/wiki/T-bone_steak" title="T-bone steak">bistecca alla fiorentina</a></i> is a large (the customary size should weigh around 1.2 to 1.5&#160;kg&#160;[40 to 50&#160;oz]) – the "date" steak – <a href="/wiki/T-bone_steak" title="T-bone steak">T-bone steak</a> of <a href="/wiki/Chianina" title="Chianina">Chianina</a> beef cooked over hot charcoal and served very rare with its more recently derived version, the <i>tagliata</i>, sliced rare beef served on a bed of <a href="/wiki/Eruca_sativa" title="Eruca sativa">arugula</a>, often with slices of <a href="/wiki/Parmigiano-Reggiano" title="Parmigiano-Reggiano">Parmesan cheese</a> on top. Most of these courses are generally served with local <a href="/wiki/Olive_oil" title="Olive oil">olive oil</a>, also a prime product enjoying a worldwide reputation.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><br />
Among the desserts, "schiacciata alla fiorentina" ("white flatbread cake") is one of the most popular; it is a very soft cake, prepared with extremely simple ingredients as it is peculiar of the florentine cuisine, and it is typically eaten on Carnival time.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Research_activity">Research activity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Florence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Research activity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:UNICEF_Innocenti_Research_Centre.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/UNICEF_Innocenti_Research_Centre.jpg/220px-UNICEF_Innocenti_Research_Centre.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/UNICEF_Innocenti_Research_Centre.jpg/330px-UNICEF_Innocenti_Research_Centre.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/UNICEF_Innocenti_Research_Centre.jpg/440px-UNICEF_Innocenti_Research_Centre.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3456" data-file-height="2592" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:UNICEF_Innocenti_Research_Centre.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Ospedale_degli_Innocenti" title="Ospedale degli Innocenti">UNICEF Innocenti Research Centre</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Research institutes and university departments are located within the Florence area and within two campuses at Polo di Novoli and Polo Scientifico di Sesto Fiorentino<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> as well as in the Research Area of <a href="/wiki/Consiglio_Nazionale_delle_Ricerche" class="mw-redirect" title="Consiglio Nazionale delle Ricerche">Consiglio Nazionale delle Ricerche</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Science_and_discovery">Science and discovery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Florence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Science and discovery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Museo_di_Storia_Naturale_di_Firenze_-_paleontology.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Museo_di_Storia_Naturale_di_Firenze_-_paleontology.JPG/220px-Museo_di_Storia_Naturale_di_Firenze_-_paleontology.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Museo_di_Storia_Naturale_di_Firenze_-_paleontology.JPG/330px-Museo_di_Storia_Naturale_di_Firenze_-_paleontology.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Museo_di_Storia_Naturale_di_Firenze_-_paleontology.JPG/440px-Museo_di_Storia_Naturale_di_Firenze_-_paleontology.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Museo_di_Storia_Naturale_di_Firenze_-_paleontology.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A display of <a href="/wiki/Proboscidean" class="mw-redirect" title="Proboscidean">proboscideans</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Museo_di_Storia_Naturale_di_Firenze" title="Museo di Storia Naturale di Firenze">Museo di Storia Naturale di Firenze</a>, or the Natural History Museum of Florence</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Florence has been an important scientific centre for centuries, notably during the Renaissance with scientists such as Leonardo da Vinci.</p>
<p>Florentines were one of the driving forces behind the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">Age of Discovery</a>. Florentine bankers financed Henry the Navigator and the Portuguese explorers who pioneered the route around Africa to India and the Far East. It was a map drawn by the Florentine <a href="/wiki/Paolo_dal_Pozzo_Toscanelli" title="Paolo dal Pozzo Toscanelli">Paolo dal Pozzo Toscanelli</a>, a student of Brunelleschi, that <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a> used to sell his "enterprise" to the Spanish monarchs, and which he used on his first voyage. Mercator's "Projection" is a refined version of Toscanelli's – taking into account the Americas, of which the Florentine was, obviously, ignorant.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo</a> and other scientists pioneered the study of optics, ballistics, astronomy, anatomy, and so on. Pico della Mirandola, Leonardo Bruni, Machiavelli, and many others laid the groundwork for our understanding of science.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fashion">Fashion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Florence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Fashion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Italian_fashion" title="Italian fashion">Italian fashion</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Fashion_designers_of_Florence" class="mw-redirect" title="Fashion designers of Florence">Fashion designers of Florence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polimoda" title="Polimoda">Polimoda</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Via_de_Tornabuoni_07.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Via_de_Tornabuoni_07.JPG/220px-Via_de_Tornabuoni_07.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Via_de_Tornabuoni_07.JPG/330px-Via_de_Tornabuoni_07.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Via_de_Tornabuoni_07.JPG/440px-Via_de_Tornabuoni_07.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2816" data-file-height="2112" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Via_de_Tornabuoni_07.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Luxury boutiques along Florence's prestigious Via de' Tornabuoni.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By the year 1300 Florence had become a centre of textile production in Europe. Many of the rich families in Renaissance Florence were major purchasers of locally produced fine clothing, and the specialists of fashion in the economy and culture of Florence during that period is often underestimated.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Florence is regarded by some as the birthplace and earliest centre of the modern (post World War Two) fashion industry in Italy. The Florentine "soirées" of the early 1950s organised by Giovanni Battista Giorgini were events where several Italian designers participated in group shows and first garnered international attention.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Florence has served as the home of the Italian fashion company <a href="/wiki/Salvatore_Ferragamo_Italia_S.p.A." class="mw-redirect" title="Salvatore Ferragamo Italia S.p.A.">Salvatore Ferragamo</a> since 1928. <a href="/wiki/Gucci" title="Gucci">Gucci</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roberto_Cavalli" title="Roberto Cavalli">Roberto Cavalli</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Emilio_Pucci" title="Emilio Pucci">Emilio Pucci</a> are also headquartered in Florence. Other major players in the fashion industry such as <a href="/wiki/Prada" title="Prada">Prada</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chanel" title="Chanel">Chanel</a> have large offices and stores in Florence or its outskirts. Florence's main upscale shopping street is <a href="/wiki/Via_de%27_Tornabuoni" title="Via de' Tornabuoni">Via de' Tornabuoni</a>, where major luxury fashion houses and jewellery labels, such as <a href="/wiki/Armani" title="Armani">Armani</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bulgari" title="Bulgari">Bulgari</a>, have their elegant boutiques. Via del Parione and Via Roma are other streets that are also well known for their high-end fashion stores.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_evocations">Historical evocations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Florence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Historical evocations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Scoppio_del_Carro"><i>Scoppio del Carro</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Florence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Scoppio del Carro">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Scoppio_del_carro" title="Scoppio del carro">Scoppio del Carro</a></i> ("Explosion of the Cart") is a celebration of the <a href="/wiki/First_Crusade" title="First Crusade">First Crusade</a>. During the day of Easter, a cart, which the Florentines call the <i>Brindellone</i> and which is led by four white oxen, is taken to the <a href="/wiki/Piazza_del_Duomo,_Florence" title="Piazza del Duomo, Florence">Piazza del Duomo</a> between the Baptistery of <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">St. John the Baptist</a> (<i>Battistero di San Giovanni</i>) and the <a href="/wiki/Florence_Cathedral" title="Florence Cathedral">Florence Cathedral</a> (<i>Santa Maria del Fiore</i>). The cart is connected by a rope to the interior of the church. Near the cart there is a model of a dove, which, according to legend, is a symbol of good luck for the city: at the end of the Easter mass, the dove emerges from the nave of the Duomo and ignites the fireworks on the cart.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Calcio_Storico"><i>Calcio Storico</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Florence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Calcio Storico">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Calcio_Storico_Corteo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Calcio_Storico_Corteo.jpg/220px-Calcio_Storico_Corteo.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Calcio_Storico_Corteo.jpg/330px-Calcio_Storico_Corteo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Calcio_Storico_Corteo.jpg/440px-Calcio_Storico_Corteo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="800" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Calcio_Storico_Corteo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Calcio Storico</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Calcio_Fiorentino" title="Calcio Fiorentino">Calcio Fiorentino</a></div>
<p><i>Calcio Storico Fiorentino</i> ("Historic Florentine <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">Football</a>"), sometimes called <i>Calcio in costume</i>, is a traditional sport, regarded as a forerunner of soccer, though the actual gameplay most closely resembles rugby. The event originates from the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, when the most important Florentine nobles amused themselves playing while wearing bright costumes. The most important match was played on 17 February 1530, during the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Florence_(1529%E2%80%931530)" class="mw-redirect" title="Siege of Florence (1529–1530)">siege of Florence</a>. That day <a href="/wiki/Papal_States" title="Papal States">Papal troops</a> besieged the city while the Florentines, with contempt of the enemies, decided to play the game notwithstanding the situation. The game is played in the Piazza di <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_Santa_Croce,_Florence" class="mw-redirect" title="Basilica of Santa Croce, Florence">Santa Croce</a>. A temporary arena is constructed, with bleachers and a sand-covered playing field. A series of matches are held between four teams representing each <i>quartiere</i> (quarter) of Florence during late June and early July.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> There are four teams: Azzurri (light blue), Bianchi (white), Rossi (red) and Verdi (green). The Azzurri are from the quarter of Santa Croce, Bianchi from the quarter of Santo Spirito, Verdi are from San Giovanni and Rossi from Santa Maria Novella.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sport">Sport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Florence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Sport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Soccer_in_Florence,_Italy,_2007.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Soccer_in_Florence%2C_Italy%2C_2007.jpg/220px-Soccer_in_Florence%2C_Italy%2C_2007.jpg" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Soccer_in_Florence%2C_Italy%2C_2007.jpg/330px-Soccer_in_Florence%2C_Italy%2C_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Soccer_in_Florence%2C_Italy%2C_2007.jpg/440px-Soccer_in_Florence%2C_Italy%2C_2007.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2816" data-file-height="1584" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Soccer_in_Florence,_Italy,_2007.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Stadio_Artemio_Franchi" title="Stadio Artemio Franchi">Stadio Artemio Franchi</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">association football</a> Florence is represented by <a href="/wiki/ACF_Fiorentina" title="ACF Fiorentina">ACF Fiorentina</a>, which plays in <a href="/wiki/Serie_A" title="Serie A">Serie A</a>, the top league of <a href="/wiki/Football_in_Italy" title="Football in Italy">Italian league system</a>. They play their games at the <a href="/wiki/Stadio_Artemio_Franchi" title="Stadio Artemio Franchi">Stadio Artemio Franchi</a>, which currently holds 47,282. The female squad of ACF Fiorentina have won the women's association football Italian Championship of the 2016-17 season.</p>
<p>The city is home of <a href="/wiki/Coverciano" title="Coverciano">Coverciano</a>, the main training ground of the <a href="/wiki/Italy_national_football_team" title="Italy national football team">Italian national team</a>, and the technical department of the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Football_Federation" title="Italian Football Federation">Italian Football Federation</a>.</p>
<p>Florence was selected to host the 2013 UCI World Road Cycling Championships.</p>
<p>Since 2017 Florence is also represented in Eccellenza, the top tier of <a href="/wiki/Rugby_union" title="Rugby union">rugby union</a> league system in Italy, by I Medicei, which is a club established in 2015 by the merging of the senior squads of I Cavalieri (of <a href="/wiki/Prato" title="Prato">Prato</a>) and Firenze Rugby 1931. I Medicei won the Serie A Championship in 2016-17 and were promoted to Eccellenza for the 2017-18 season.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/RN_Florentia" title="RN Florentia">Rari Nantes Florentia</a> is a successful <a href="/wiki/Water_polo" title="Water polo">water polo</a> club based in Florence; both its male and female squads have won several Italian Championship and the femal squad has also European titles in their palmarès.</p>

</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tramvia_firenze_sirio.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Tramvia_firenze_sirio.jpg/220px-Tramvia_firenze_sirio.jpg" width="220" height="191" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Tramvia_firenze_sirio.jpg/330px-Tramvia_firenze_sirio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Tramvia_firenze_sirio.jpg/440px-Tramvia_firenze_sirio.jpg 2x" data-file-width="450" data-file-height="390" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tramvia_firenze_sirio.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/List_of_town_tramway_systems_in_Italy" title="List of town tramway systems in Italy">Tramway</a> <a href="/wiki/Sirio" title="Sirio">Sirio</a> in Florence</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The principal public transport network within the city is run by the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.ataf.net/Default.aspx?LN=en-US">ATAF and Li-nea</a> bus company, with tickets available at local tobacconists, bars and newspaper stalls. Individual tickets, or a pass called the Carta Agile with multiple rides (10, 21 or 35), may be used on ATAF&amp;Li-nea buses, Tramvia, and 2nd class local trains, only within city railway stations. Once on the bus, the rider must stamp tickets (or swipe the Carta Agile) by using the machines on board, unlike train tickets, which must be validated before boarding. The main bus station is next to Santa Maria Novella <a href="/wiki/Railway_station" class="mw-redirect" title="Railway station">railway station</a>. <a href="/wiki/Trenitalia" title="Trenitalia">Trenitalia</a> runs trains between the railway stations within the city, and to other destinations around Italy and Europe. The central railway station, <a href="/wiki/Firenze_Santa_Maria_Novella_railway_station" title="Firenze Santa Maria Novella railway station">Santa Maria Novella railway station</a>, is about 500&#160;m (1,600&#160;ft) northwest of the Piazza del Duomo. There are two other important stations: Campo Di Marte and Rifredi. Most bundled routes are Firenze—Pisa, Firenze—Viareggio and Firenze-Arezzo (along the main line to Rome). Other local railways connect Florence with <a href="/wiki/Borgo_San_Lorenzo" title="Borgo San Lorenzo">Borgo San Lorenzo</a> in the Mugello area (Faentina railway) and <a href="/wiki/Siena" title="Siena">Siena</a>.</p>
<p>Long distance 10&#160;km (6.21&#160;mi) buses are run by the SITA, Copit, CAP companies. The transit companies also accommodate travellers from the Amerigo Vespucci Airport, which is 5&#160;km (3.1&#160;mi) west of the city centre, and which has scheduled services run by major European carriers.</p>
<p>The centre of the city is closed to through-traffic, although buses, taxis and residents with appropriate permits are allowed in. This area is commonly referred to as the ZTL (<i>Zona Traffico Limitato</i>), which is divided into five subsections.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Residents of one section, therefore, will only be able to drive in their district and perhaps some surrounding ones. Cars without permits are allowed to enter after 7.30&#160;pm, or before 7.30&#160;am. The rules shift during the tourist-filled summers, putting more restrictions on where one can get in and out.</p>
<p>In an effort to reduce air pollution and car traffic in the city, a multi-line tram network called <i>Tramvia</i> is under construction. The first line began operation on 14 February 2010 and connects Florence's primary intercity railway station (<a href="/wiki/Firenze_Santa_Maria_Novella_railway_station" title="Firenze Santa Maria Novella railway station">Santa Maria Novella</a>) with the southwestern suburb of <a href="/wiki/Scandicci" title="Scandicci">Scandicci</a>. This line is 7.4&#160;km (4.6&#160;mi) long and has 14 stops. The construction of a second line began on 5 November 2011, construction was stopped due to contractors' difficulties but should restart in a few months, completion is now previewed in 2017. This second line will connect Florence's airport with the city centre. A third line (from <a href="/wiki/Firenze_Santa_Maria_Novella_railway_station" title="Firenze Santa Maria Novella railway station">Santa Maria Novella</a> to the Careggi area, where are the most important hospitals of Florence) has gained governmental approval, its construction will follow the second line's timeline.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Florence_Public_Transportation_Statistics">Florence Public Transportation Statistics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Florence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Florence Public Transportation Statistics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The average amount of time people spend commuting with public transit in Firenze, for example to and from work, on a weekday is 59 min. 13% of public transit riders, ride for more than 2 hours every day. The average amount of time people wait at a stop or station for public transit is 14 min, while 22% of riders wait for over 20 minutes on average every day. The average distance people usually ride in a single trip with public transit is 4.1&#160;km, while 3% travel for over 12&#160;km in a single direction.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Railway_station">Railway station</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Florence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Railway station">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Firenze_Santa_Maria_Novella_railway_station" title="Firenze Santa Maria Novella railway station">Firenze Santa Maria Novella railway station</a> is the main national and international railway station in Florence and is used by 59&#160;million people every year.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> The building, designed by Giovanni Michelucci, was built in the <i>Italian Rationalism</i> style and it is one of the major rationalist buildings in Italy. It is located in <i>Piazza della Stazione</i>, near the <a href="/wiki/Fortezza_da_Basso" title="Fortezza da Basso">Fortezza da Basso</a> (a masterpiece of the military Renaissance architecture<sup id="cite_ref-city-getaway_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-city-getaway-87">[87]</a></sup>) and the <a href="/wiki/Viali_di_Circonvallazione" title="Viali di Circonvallazione">Viali di Circonvallazione</a>, and in front of the <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_Santa_Maria_Novella" class="mw-redirect" title="Basilica of Santa Maria Novella">Basilica of Santa Maria Novella</a>'s apse from which it takes its name.</p>
<p>A new <a href="/wiki/High-speed_rail" title="High-speed rail">high-speed rail</a> station is under construction and is contracted to be operational by 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> It is planned to be connected to Vespucci airport, Santa Maria Novella railway station, and to the city centre by the second line of Tramvia.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> The architectural firms <a href="/wiki/Foster_and_Partners" title="Foster and Partners">Foster + Partners</a> and Lancietti Passaleva Giordo and Associates designed this new rail station.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Airport">Airport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Florence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Airport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Florence_-_Peretola_(Amerigo_Vespucci)_(FLR_-_LIRQ)_AN0591245.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Florence_-_Peretola_%28Amerigo_Vespucci%29_%28FLR_-_LIRQ%29_AN0591245.jpg/220px-Florence_-_Peretola_%28Amerigo_Vespucci%29_%28FLR_-_LIRQ%29_AN0591245.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Florence_-_Peretola_%28Amerigo_Vespucci%29_%28FLR_-_LIRQ%29_AN0591245.jpg/330px-Florence_-_Peretola_%28Amerigo_Vespucci%29_%28FLR_-_LIRQ%29_AN0591245.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Florence_-_Peretola_%28Amerigo_Vespucci%29_%28FLR_-_LIRQ%29_AN0591245.jpg/440px-Florence_-_Peretola_%28Amerigo_Vespucci%29_%28FLR_-_LIRQ%29_AN0591245.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1001" data-file-height="667" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Florence_-_Peretola_(Amerigo_Vespucci)_(FLR_-_LIRQ)_AN0591245.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei_Airport" class="mw-redirect" title="Galileo Galilei Airport">Florence Airport</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Florence_Airport" title="Florence Airport">Florence Airport</a>, Peretola, is one of two main airports in the Tuscany region, the other being <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei_Airport" class="mw-redirect" title="Galileo Galilei Airport">Galileo Galilei International Airport</a> in Pisa.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_residents">Notable residents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Florence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Notable residents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li><a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Boccaccio" title="Giovanni Boccaccio">Giovanni Boccaccio</a>, poet</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Baldassarre_Bonaiuti" title="Baldassarre Bonaiuti">Baldassarre Bonaiuti</a>, 14th-century chronicler</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sandro_Botticelli" title="Sandro Botticelli">Sandro Botticelli</a>, painter</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Aureliano_Brandolini" title="Aureliano Brandolini">Aureliano Brandolini</a>, agronomist and development cooperation scholar</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Robert_Browning" title="Robert Browning">Robert Browning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Barrett_Browning" title="Elizabeth Barrett Browning">Elizabeth Barrett Browning</a>, 19th-century English poets</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Filippo_Brunelleschi" title="Filippo Brunelleschi">Filippo Brunelleschi</a>, architect</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Michelangelo" title="Michelangelo">Michelangelo</a> Buonarroti, sculptor, painter, author of the <a href="/wiki/Sistine_Chapel_ceiling" title="Sistine Chapel ceiling">ceiling of the Sistine Chapel</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/David_(Michelangelo)" title="David (Michelangelo)">David</a></i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Francesco_Casagrande" title="Francesco Casagrande">Francesco Casagrande</a>, cyclist</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Roberto_Cavalli" title="Roberto Cavalli">Roberto Cavalli</a>, fashion designer</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Carlo_Collodi" title="Carlo Collodi">Carlo Collodi</a>, writer</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Enrico_Coveri" title="Enrico Coveri">Enrico Coveri</a>, fashion designer</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Donatello" title="Donatello">Donatello</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sculpture" title="Sculpture">sculptor</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Oriana_Fallaci" title="Oriana Fallaci">Oriana Fallaci</a>, journalist and author</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Salvatore_Ferragamo" title="Salvatore Ferragamo">Salvatore Ferragamo</a>, fashion designer and shoemaker</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mike_Francis_(musician)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mike Francis (musician)">Mike Francis (musician)</a> born Francesco Puccioni, singer and composer</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Silpa_Bhirasri" title="Silpa Bhirasri">Silpa Bhirasri</a> (born Corrado Feroci), sculptor, credited as the principal figure of modern art in Thailand.<sup id="cite_ref-su_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-su-101">[101]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Frescobaldi" title="Frescobaldi">Frescobaldi Family</a>, notable bankers and wine producers</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a>, Italian physicist, astronomer, and philosopher</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Giotto" title="Giotto">Giotto</a>, early 14th-century painter, sculptor and architect</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lorenzo_Ghiberti" title="Lorenzo Ghiberti">Lorenzo Ghiberti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sculpture" title="Sculpture">sculptor</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Guccio_Gucci" title="Guccio Gucci">Guccio Gucci</a>, founder of the <a href="/wiki/Gucci" title="Gucci">Gucci</a> label</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Robert_Lowell" title="Robert Lowell">Robert Lowell</a>, poet</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Niccol%C3%B2_Machiavelli" title="Niccolò Machiavelli">Niccolò Machiavelli</a>, poet, philosopher and political thinker, author of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Prince" title="The Prince">The Prince</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Discourses_on_Livy" title="Discourses on Livy">The Discourses</a></i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Masaccio" title="Masaccio">Masaccio</a>, painter</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Rose_McGowan" title="Rose McGowan">Rose McGowan</a>, Florence-born actress</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/House_of_Medici" title="House of Medici">Medici</a> family</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Girolamo_Mei" title="Girolamo Mei">Girolamo Mei</a>, historian and humanist</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Antonio_Meucci" title="Antonio Meucci">Antonio Meucci</a>, <a href="/wiki/Invention_of_the_telephone" title="Invention of the telephone">inventor of the telephone</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Florence_Nightingale" title="Florence Nightingale">Florence Nightingale</a>, pioneer of modern nursing, and statistician</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Virginia_Oldoini" class="mw-redirect" title="Virginia Oldoini">Virginia Oldoini</a>, Countess of Castiglione, early photographic artist, secret agent and courtesan</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Valerio_Profondavalle" title="Valerio Profondavalle">Valerio Profondavalle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flanders" title="Flanders">Flemish</a> painter</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a>, painter</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Anna_Sarfatti" title="Anna Sarfatti">Anna Sarfatti</a>, children's author</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Girolamo_Savonarola" title="Girolamo Savonarola">Girolamo Savonarola</a>, reformist</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Adriana_Seroni" title="Adriana Seroni">Adriana Seroni</a>, politician</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Spadolini" title="Giovanni Spadolini">Giovanni Spadolini</a>, politician</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Antonio_Squarcialupi" title="Antonio Squarcialupi">Antonio Squarcialupi</a>, organist and composer</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Evangelista_Torricelli" title="Evangelista Torricelli">Evangelista Torricelli</a>, Italian physicist</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Giorgio_Vasari" title="Giorgio Vasari">Giorgio Vasari</a>, painter, architect, and historian</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Amerigo_Vespucci" title="Amerigo Vespucci">Amerigo Vespucci</a>, explorer and cartographer, namesake of the Americas</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polymath" title="Polymath">polymath</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lisa_del_Giocondo" title="Lisa del Giocondo">Lisa del Giocondo</a>, model of the <a href="/wiki/Mona_Lisa" title="Mona Lisa">Mona Lisa</a></li>
</ul>

<th scope="row" class="navbox-group" style="width:1%"><a href="/wiki/Northwest_Italy" title="Northwest Italy">Northwest</a></th>
<td class="navbox-list navbox-odd" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px">
<div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Crespi_d%27Adda" title="Crespi d'Adda">Crespi d'Adda</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Genoa" title="Genoa">Genoa</a></li>
